#### ↳ Re: variable length fields for flexibility in subroutines

- **From:** Robert Jones <rjones0@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-23T13:55:21-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185224121.375977.216160@r34g2000hsd.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<46A49D15.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<46A49D15.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
On Jul 23, 7:20 pm, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbr...@efirstbank.com>
wrote:
> Wow!  A post about COBOL!
>
…[140 more quoted lines elided]…
> ApplicationsÌFirstBank Data Corporation ,, Lakewood, CO  USA

I have not used DOS/VSE for a long time and it was relatively
restrictive then, however some thoughts follow:

1) If you are using call by reference, then any individual 01 linkage
definitions in the subroutine can be as large as you like (within
compiler limits) without actually occupying any storage, so you could
make them much larger than the calling program's llnkage parameters,
so long as you have a mechanism in the subroutine to prevent the
referencing of data beyond the end of the area passed by the calling
program.  As you say, a length field can be used for this purpose.

2) In the 2002 standard there is an OPTIONAL parameter that can be
used with the USING phrase of the Procedure Division header, but I
douht that your compiler provides it, the z/OS V3.4 compiler
doesn't!   But you might consider it as a method for the future.
Perhaps by designing your subroutine in a way that would facilitate
its use when it is available, perhaps in a few years time.

3)  I think that you can have more 01 level linkage parameters in your
subroutine than are in the calling program, provided you don't
reference them.  You could devise a mechanism to pass the number of
parameters in the first parameter, to tell the subroutine which ones
it can use.  The correspondence between parameters in the calling and
called programs is purely sequential, (the naming of the parameters is
irrelevant for this purpose), so at least one mandatory parameter must
be first in the list to tell the subroutine how many it is allowed to
access.

Hope this helps

Robert
```

```
<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>, Lizette
Koehler<starsoul@mindspring.com> wrote:
> I have just joined and am trying to determine what caused 1 paragraph
> out of many to do a fall though as opposed to going back up and
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> code.  Such  PERFORM with EXIT and IF  END-IF.

One thing you can look at is the 'level number' (the SL column) in the
compile listing.

Take, for example, this very contrived example:

000008         000008     IF A > 4        
000009      1  000009         CONTINUE    
000010         000010     ELSE            
000011      1  000011     IF A > 3        
000012      2  000012         CONTINUE    
000013      1  000013     ELSE            
000014      2  000014     IF A > 2        
000015      3  000015         CONTINUE    
000016      2  000016     ELSE            
000017      3  000017     IF A > 1        
000018      4  000018         CONTINUE    
000019      3  000019     ELSE            
000020      4  000020         PERFORM DOIT
000021      3  000021     END-IF          
000022      2  000022     END-IF          
000023      1  000023     END-IF          
000024         000024     END-IF          

As you can see, the first IF (the one with no level number) is terminated by
the final END-IF.  If there is a level number next to what appears to be the
'final' END-IF then you can be pretty certain and END-IF is missing
somewhere.

Though I'm not sure off the top of my head how a missing END-IF could cause
the symptoms you are experiencing.  Since a paragraph *must* end with a full
stop (period) then, at the very least, any previous IF statement would be
terminated at that point.  Is it possible that you are falling through to
the beginning of the perform thru group?  For example:

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
START-HERE.
    PERFORM BEGIN-PARA THRU EXIT-PARA.

BEGIN-PARA.
    DISPLAY 'BEGINNING'.

MIDDLE-PARA.
    DISPLAY 'MIDDLE'.

EXIT-PARA.
    EXIT.

DROPPED-THRU.
    DISPLAY 'UNEXPECTED?'

This is too obvious an error to make (missing a STOP RUN at the end of the
START-HERE paragraph), but something like it is often the cause of this kind
of problem.  Of course it has the obvious symptom (in this case) of the
output being:
BEGINNING
MIDDLE
BEGINNING
MIDDLE
UNEXPECTED?
But if your code isn't doing displays or something else that makes it easy
to see, you might miss it.

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T12:36:59-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jul 24, 8:38 am, Lizette Koehler <stars...@mindspring.com> wrote:

> I have just joined and am trying to determine what caused 1 paragraph
> out of many to do a fall though as opposed to going back up and
> executing the next instruction.  I suspect that it has something to do
> with a missint END-IF/

It is very unlikely to be 'missing END-IF'.

If you have code that is

    PERFORM A THRU A-exit
    ..

   A.
     ...
        GO TO A-exit
     ...
   A-exit.

then there are several programming errors available to you to cause a
drop-thru.

1) forgetting the THRU:-  "PERFORM A"
   The GO TO A-Exit will take you beyond the scope of the perform.

2) Using the wrong exit:- "PERFORM A THRU B-exit"
   The code will drop thru until it finds B-exit somewhere.

3) GO TO the wrong exit:- "GO TO X-exit"
   In A a GO TO outside the range will cause mysterious action.

4) Duplicate exit paragraph:- Two A-exit paragraphs.
   The GO TO goes to the wrong one.

The main problem is that the compiler cannot tell you that these
errors exist. The checking must be done manually and exhaustively or
by a program processing the code and checking against a set of
restrictive standards.

Being lazy and not wanting to do this type of checking I avoid all
need for it by using code that never requires it. ie No SECTIONs, no
GOTO, no THRU, no EXIT PERFORM/PARAGRAPH/SECTION, no NEXT SENTENCE.
```

```
On Jul 28, 12:03 pm, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:

> Man, don't start a religious style war again.  SECTIONS are never necessary,
> so far as I know, but can be used perfectly effectively.
>
> Evrybody has a verb or two that they dislike.  Who cares????  Does it work?

SECTIONs do have their uses, for example segmentation. As I pointed
out above I dislike things that can easily lead to errors and that the
compiler won't tell you about. In fact I would use that as a
definition of what is _not_ 'easy to maintain' and _not_ 'perfectly
effective'.

For example (as above) the simple absence of one SECTION word on a
label can cause a drop thru error that can be difficult to locate and
which the compiler gives no warning of.

The only purpose for using PERFORMed SECTIONs instead of having all
the labels as paragraphs is to allow the use of exit paragraphs and
the associated GO TOs. Thus this style suffers the errors in my other
messages of this topic, ones that the compiler cannot find for you.

Perhaps you find that these errors are simply a part of 'normal
programming' and find them easy, after all what would you do for the
rest of the week if they did not occur.

> Does it follow the installation standards?
> Discussion over!

Yes, that is often used to protect poor programming styles.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T23:42:30-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
[snip]
> The only purpose for using PERFORMed SECTIONs instead of having all
> the labels as paragraphs is to allow the use of exit paragraphs and
> the associated GO TOs.

Well, no, that is not the 'only' purpose! Another is
encapsulating mini-programs as parts of a larger program.
This is similar to nested programs; but without the call
overhead or the structuring requirements for contained
and common programs. The first paragraph terminates
with EXIT SECTION rather than EXIT PROGRAM
and all paragraphs, except the first, within each section
are performed only by other paragraphs within the same
section; though some statements may perform other
sections. It is EXIT SECTION that allow this to be
accomplished without exit paragraphs or GO TO
statements. <g>

I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
the code for a recursive quicksort.
-----
       quick-sort section.
            ...
           call "qsort" using i j
           exit section
           .
           entry "qsort" using l r
            ...
           exit program
           .
        [performed paragraphs]
        [next section]
-----
The implementation does not allow recursive nested
programs.

[Incidentally, this is the same program, I had mentioned
some time ago, as having one section where only GO TO
statements were used for flow control within that section.]
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

```
On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> statements. <g>

EXIT SECTION/PARAGRAPH are just GO TOs to an unnamed paragraph.

You could accomplish exactly the same thing without the SECTION word
and without the EXIT SECTION. A comment line could indicate that this
was a group of related paragraphs.

That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
labels (and comments).  It does however allow the use of a GO TO
within the scope of the PERFORM though you have replaced this with a
_slightly_ better EXIT SECTION.

I like to move code from one place to another. For example taking it
out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
down a nested IF into small paragraphs. Any statement that does not
allow this or needs to be changed when moving code I do not use.

For example in your first paragraph if you had a nested IF that had an
EXIT SECTION it would be an error to move that code into a paragraph
(or another SECTION) and PERFORM it.

>
> I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> statements were used for flow control within that section.]

And in what way does this make it 'more maintainable' ?
```

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com...
> On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> EXIT SECTION/PARAGRAPH are just GO TOs to an unnamed paragraph.

There are technical reasons why the various EXITs are
not "just GO TOs". I know because it was one of my
contributions to finalizing the 2002 standard. The
introduction of the "unnamed paragraph" as the target
for EXIT SECTION was necessary to distinguish
between the transfer mechanism at the end of the last
paragraph of the section and the one at the end of the
section, itself. These are the transfer mechanisms used
by PERFORM, for example. That the EXITs are seen
as "just GO TOs" seems to show how well the
technicalities were addressed.

> You could accomplish exactly the same thing without the SECTION word
> and without the EXIT SECTION. A comment line could indicate that this
> was a group of related paragraphs.

Others, maybe; but not me! I would accomplish it in
exactly the same way because I am comfortable with
sections and EXIT SECTION.

> That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
> any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
> labels (and comments).  It does however allow the use of a GO TO
> within the scope of the PERFORM though you have replaced this with a
> _slightly_ better EXIT SECTION.

Well, as previously discussed, one reason I use sections
is to allow the use of GO TO; but only within the section.
Thus the section exists as if it were a capsule; such that
GO TO statements occur only within that which is
encapsulated.

> I like to move code from one place to another. For example taking it
> out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> (or another SECTION) and PERFORM it.

If I used EXIT SECTION in an IF, EVALUATE, or other
conditional statement, it would not likely be nested and,
therefore, would not need to moved.

    IF  condition
        EXIT SECTION
    END-IF

> >
> > I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> And in what way does this make it 'more maintainable' ?

When sections are used as mini-programs, they have
a well-defined interface, thus all changes to the
mini-program are isolated from the rest of the code.
And, such mini-programs may be easier to re-use.

When sections are used in the same manner as
paragraphs, they do not necessarily have what may
qualify as an interface and the lack of isolation allows
for the same ad hoc coupling that may exit with
paragraphs only.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

No! In particular, paragraph-names need only be unique
within a section. Others may use paragraph-names that
are unique within the program (for example, a prefix on
every procedure-name) and thus obtain some degreee
of equivalence. Since I am not concerned, generally, with
paragraph-names outside the section, any attempt to
modify "my" programs to remove the section keyword
could result in paragraph-name conflicts.

I don't know that sections "would make the compiler do
better checking"; but I believe it helps me to do better
writing.

> > > That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
> > > any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> impose with no help from the language or compiler.

Except that the compiler will resolve conflicting
paragraph-names to the same section containing the
GO TO statement.

> > > I like to move code from one place to another. For example taking it
> > > out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> manual intervention for what I see as trivial restructuring.

Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
EXIT SECTION may occur anywhere within the section to
the same effect. It is moving EXIT PARAGRAPH that may
create some problems; but EXIT PARAGRAPH is unrelated
to the use of sections.

> > > > I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
> > > > the code for a recursive quicksort.
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> they use.

Paragraph-names are isolated. Isolation of local or
private data requires a naming convention.

> > When sections are used in the same manner as
> > paragraphs, they do not necessarily have what may
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> merely the way you order the code.

Well, there is a difference in regard to paragraph-names.
And, ...

    move data to section-interface-input
    perform section-name
    move section-interface-output to data

is as real an interface as the equivalent call to a separate
program. It is in the defining of the interface as actual
data description entries that makes the interface real
and, while I suppose some discipline may be required to
prevent ad hoc coupling, I see it as less of an imposition
when using sections. I think this is because procedural
programming is about procedures and using sections as
major procedures implemented by paragraphs as minor
procedures stands out better than paragraph groups.
```

```
On Jul 30, 2:25 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> within a section.

I will grant you that paragraph names only need be unique within a
section and that it is not necessary to qualify the name. However,
this need not be a 'good thing', the compiler may not warn about
'missing' or misspelled labels. For example if two sections were
supposed to have the same paragraph name but one became corrupted or
was misspelled then the code would silently use the wrong one. There
is no actual 'encapsulation'.

I would also say that this was a really useful feature in the 60s when
huge monolithic programs had to be built by several programmers
because CALL was not available (it was added to ANS'74). Now there are
actual mechanisms that do real encapsulation.

> Others may use paragraph-names that
> are unique within the program (for example, a prefix on
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> could result in paragraph-name conflicts.

At least the compiler would tell you where those were.

> I don't know that sections "would make the compiler do
> better checking"; but I believe it helps me to do better
> writing.

Yes, encapsulated code and defined interfaces is better writing.
Grouping related code is better writing. But you are not using any
compiler features to enforce this as would happen with sub-programs or
classes, it is better writing by manually imposed discipline.

> Except that the compiler will resolve conflicting
> paragraph-names to the same section containing the
> GO TO statement.

Some years ago I had sent a proposal to J4 that suggested the addition
of label qualifiers. For example adding a LOCAL qualifier to a
paragraph label would ensure that it could only ever be referenced
from within the enclosing section *. While, as you say, the compiler
will resolve first of all to the same section, if by error or
misspelling it does not exist, the compiler will then resolve to
wherever it can can find the name and this may not cause an warning or
error.

In any case I no longer regard 'GO TO' as better programming, not
since ANS'85.

> Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
> versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> to the use of sections.

You are probably correct. I decided years ago that these were
unnecessary in the style of coding that I use and were merely another
way of doing a GOTO while pretending not to. In any case it is
unlikely that I will ever use a 2002 compiler.

> > But is only a manually imposed so-called 'mini-program'. There is
> > nothing in the language that assists them. as for example is done with
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> private data requires a naming convention.

No. Paragraph names are _not_ actually isolated. There is nothing
preventing code in one section to perform a paragraph in another. It
may be that the compiler will attempt to resolve to a local name
first.

> > Sticking the word SECTION on it makes zero difference, it is only your
> > self-imposed discipline that creates this alleged 'isolation' and lack
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>     move section-interface-output to data

> is as real an interface as the equivalent call to a separate
> program.

No more so than PERFORM paragraph-name where that was the same first
paragraph to a related group of paragraphs. But then you wouldn't be
able to GOTO xx-exit or exit section, which is the difference that I
said in the first place.

> It is in the defining of the interface as actual
> data description entries that makes the interface real

Exactly, it has nothing to do with the use of the SECTION word.

> and, while I suppose some discipline may be required to
> prevent ad hoc coupling, I see it as less of an imposition
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> procedures stands out better than paragraph groups.

I think that mixing performs of sections with perform of paragraphs is
poor programming and I have no problem grouping paragraphs together
into related groups without using the SECTION word.

I can think of many features that would assist in ensuring this but
the word SECTION does none of them.


* The qualifiers that I proposed (that I can recall) were FROM,
PERFORMED, ABOVE, LOCAL.

LOCAL meant only accessible from within the section. FROM meant it
could be subject of a GO TO. ABOVE meant it could be dropped into from
the previous paragraph. PERFORMED meant it could be performed. If any
qualifier(s) were used it restricted the program to those specified.
Example:

      ABC SECTION PERFORMED.
      ABC-1 ABOVE LOCAL.
           ...
           GO TO ABC-Exit.
      ABC-2 PERFORMED LOCAL.
           ...
      ABC-Exit FROM LOCAL.
         EXIT.

ABC could only be performed. Drop thru into it would cause run time
error.
Drop thru into ABC-2 or ABC-exit also run time error.
GO TO ABC-exit from anywhere outside the section is compiler error.
ABC-1 can only be dropped into (from the perform of ABC).

However, J4 quite rightly rejected this as there were better ways of
encapsulating.
```

```
On Sun, 29 Jul 2007 10:25:28 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:

>Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
>versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>to the use of sections.

The primary thing that EXIT PARAGRAPH does in the discussion of
SECTIONs is to get rid of the argument that sometimes maintenance is
easier with a GO TO MY-EXIT command. 

So now we don't need any type of PERFORM THRU commands.  All of the
programming of SECTIONS that I have seen since around 1980 have been
for implicit PERFORM THRU commands, not counting the old SORT
requirement.

When we get rid of all implied or explicit PERFORM THRU, never
dropping from paragraph to paragraph, then paragraph position doesn't
matter except for the very first paragraph.   

With this philosophy, my compiler can mean some ugly switches when a
maintenance programmer wants to get out of a paragraph with minimal
programming changes - but yours doesn't have this problem.
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:s6fsa35iirm4ehc1gr23r4e9i2jj9m32ib@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 30 Jul 2007 15:07:35 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> reasons such as memory segmentation, and sorts).

It seems that our experiences are different, which may
explain why you have questions.

> This thread proposed that it should also be used to achieve some of
> the advantages of subprograms or OO, by counting on programmers being
> disciplined.    In that case, why not use subprograms or OO?

The value of program, function, and method definitions
lies in the ability to use the resulting entities more than
once. The question then is how is one to determine what
common functionality to include in those separate entities.
One may use analysis techniques for some cases,
particularly for those that are recognizable; and one may
use design techniques for those that are not
well-understood. Using design as the phase for creating
and describing these "mini-programs" may result in more
reusable code, once the common functionality emerges.

I also mentioned "ad hoc coupling". This goes hand-in-hand
with ad hoc design. How is one to determine what interfaces
and functionality may be present if everything is the result of
"because I felt like it". This includes placing code into separate
entities "because it seems a good thing to do".
```

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-29T23:56:46+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com>`

```


"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message 
news:13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com...
>> On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

<snipped rehash of old debate ...:-)>
>
> When sections are used as mini-programs, they have
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I was impressed with these two paragraphs.

The first one is something I do (did?) all the time. It is particularly 
obvious when you use OO, although I hadn't thought about it in the terms 
described.

I think the second paragraph may have identified why there is such divided 
opinion over the use of SECTION. Many people see it in the same terms they 
see a paragraph name. Those of us who have used it successfully for many 
years (without being shot, or even frowned at, by people subsequently 
required to maintain our code), do not use it in that fashion.

I have never used PERFORM XXX-SECTION THRU xx-EXIT and I never would. Using 
SECTION makes the THRU redundant.

As for cutting and pasting bits of source code around a program, no I don't 
do that either. If you need it in more than one place, put it in a SECTION 
and PERFORM it (Or consider making it a called subprogram).

I think this second paragraph is an insightful comment on use of SECTIONS. 
(Given that this topic has been thrashed to death here over the years, this 
is the first time I have seen anything posted that gave a different insight 
or actually added something of value.)

For myself, I was forced to move from PERFORM THRU (paragraph names) to 
PERFORM [Section] when the standards in the place I was working at were 
changed by a new chief programmer who liked SECTIONS. (This was around 
1968). Very explicit rules were laid down about their use (no mixing 
PERFORMs of paragraphs and sections in the same program, no use of THRU, 
Sections being used to encapsulate specific functions, and so on. Most of us 
were not happy with the change; people usually resist what they're not used 
to, but within a few months most of us had got the "hang of it" and had no 
problem with it. Since then I have always written SECTIONed code in COBOL 
and it has never been a problem (for me, or for others who had to 
subsequently maintain the code.)

Yes, it CAN be problematic (so can many COBOL constructs when used loosely) 
but it can also be useful.

Why it is necessary to have a jihad over it or call for the shooting of 
people who do it, is beyond me...:-) I have mantained code written by others 
that used every kind of variation  and I don't recall ever complaining about 
it. That's what they paid me to do. I'm a professional programmer; give me 
code that's broken and I'll fix it.(There was a time when I wasn't the only 
one.... :-))

So often here I see people bleating about "ease of maintenance" when what 
they really mean is "Write it the way I do, then it 's easy to maintain"... 
They don't usually add the "...for me" that that particular sentence 
actually requires.

Oh, did I mention I also maintained code that had ALTERs in it?  Never had a 
problem with that particular construct, either, and on early platforms where 
we cared about bytes and performance, ALTER was an extremely efficient way 
of implementing things like first time switches and managing overlays. I was 
sorry to see it go, but the wimps won the day... :-)

I have become persuaded against GO TO over the years, but it really doesn't 
bother me if others use it. After maintaining and managing spaghetti code 
for years before Mr. Djikstra wrote his paper, I was pretty blase about it.

These days components rather than source code are more the focus of my 
effort, so I really don't care about source code wars, or people 
pontificating on what is "right" or "wrong" regarding technique, in a 
near-obsolete programming language.

PL got it right when he says that  what works is what matters.

Anyway, thanks to Rick, whose post shed some light on WHY this is a 
sensitive topic.

Pete.

"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```
On Jul 31, 2:38 pm, LX-i <lxi0...@netscape.net> wrote:

> OK - here's a question for you old guy... er, seasoned professionals...
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> ALTER in a good way?

At the time of Cobol being designed in the late 50s it was quite
common for assembler programmers to use self-modifying code as a way
of avoiding the overhead of testing flags. In fact with the first
machines I used the PERFORM actually did work by modifying a branch at
the end of a PERFORM scope to jump back to after the PERFORM and then
branched to the start of the scope. The 'EXIT' noise word in its own
paragraph was required in very early compilers because that reserved
space for the branch that the PERFORM set.

ALTER was implementing the standard mechanism in common use.

If you want to see something complicated try to get the book 'Early
British Computers' where it describes programming for mercury delay
tubes and gives an example.

Because the cycle time of the memory was much slower than the
instruction execution speed it was necessary to do several
instructions in one memory cycle.  So every instruction specified the
next instruction address, it would be far to slow to have to wait for
the next address to come round again. 10 would be, say, an add of
address 15 to the 'mill' and next instruction at 30. The instruction
would be able to complete this and pick up the value, complete the add
and pick up 30. 30 would store the accumulator in 38 and next
instruction 47.

The program had to be laid out in a map and timings done to get it to
work at best speed. Core store allowing sequential instructions and
self-modifying code (eg ALTER) was an enormous improvement in
programming.
```

```
LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com...

An example that struck me as sensible at the time was to handle EOF in an
out-of-line file read: it's a long time ago so I don't recall the exact
details but it was something like this:

READ.

READ FILE AT END MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO FILE-KEY
                                   ALTER READ TO PROCEED TO READ-X
                                    GO TO READ-X.
(A bunch of other code)
READ-X.
EXIT.

As I say I can't recall the details; this construct did solve whatever the
problem statement was and did not cause too much strain on the maintainer
since the target of the ALTER was right there.  I do remember being a bit
nervous about the compiler's ability to handle the paragraph-name READ both
as a PERFORM target and an ALTER target, but there were never any problems.

PL
```

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

- **Message-ID:** `<1185913066.280005.293910@i13g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com> <5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Aug 1, 4:48 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> If, instead, you were to formulate it as:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> [snip]

> Can you honestly say that the second example above is much more complex than
> the first? Or so "horrendous" it couldn't be maintained by a trained COBOL
> Programmer? :-)

Yes, it is actually far more complex than the first.

It is more complex because it has added a temporal element into the
code. Although it appears to be static it changes with time during the
execution. While 'First-Time' has an apparently fixed and obvious
destination the actual destination varies from time to time.

It is fairly obvious when reading this few lines that it does change
at some point to a different destination. What is not apparent is that
it may be changed by other code elsewhere to somewhere completely
different, or back to its original setting.

The only way to determine how the program will actually run with any
particular set of data is the step through every instruction starting
from the top and recording which way the ALTERs set the paths as the
data is processed.

I once posted a proposed method of quantifying one type of complexity
in Cobol code. Basically it was a summation of the number of ways that
each label could be reached. For each label (paragraph or section) add
1 if it can be dropped through, and 1 if it subject to a GO TO and 1
if it is subject of a PERFORM and 1 if it is subject of a THRU.

For each label that is subject to an ALTER add 10,000.

As an aside one could see that the measurement could be reduced by,
say, adding an explicit GO TO ~exit at the end of the paragraph just
before this to eliminate the drop through.

eg    PERFORM A THRU A-exit   (or A section)

      A (or section)
         IF ... GO TO A-exit.

         GO TO A-exit.
      A-exit.
         EXIT.

This additional and 'unnecessary' GO TO eliminates the drop through
and thus reduces the 'complexity count' to 3 (if THRU) or 2 (if
section, though a first pargraph label would add 1 as a drop through).

This is reasonable because adding a paragraph before A-exit is now no
longer dropped through accidentally. The complexity of maintaining the
program is reduced by eliminating opportunities for mistakes.

Changing the GO TO A-exit to EXIT PARGRAPH/SECTION, or eliminating all
these by using better (IMHO) IF .. ELSE logic and scope terminators
reduces the 'complexity count'  further.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T21:18:05-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185941885.429033.151800@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<5h9tuqF3ja9auU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com> <5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net> <1185913066.280005.293910@i13g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <5h9tuqF3ja9auU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Aug 1, 11:12 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> I don't intend to arguye your comments on "complexity" Richard, other than
> to say it is all relative and subjective. (Even though you are attempting to
> "calculate it" objectively.)

So, do you think that my assigning 10,000 as the complexity factor for
ALTER was not objective ?

I think that is exactly how many times that I would object to an
ALTER.   ;-)


> The fact that the statement COULD be switched from anywhere in the program
> to anywhere else, doesn't mean it WILL be...
>
> And even if it was, statements must appear in source to do it and these can
> be tracked.

Yes, it certainly will be within the source, but that is a point I
have made before.  If, for example, GO TOs are allowed from any point
in a program then the only way to know how a small piece of the
program works is by examining the whole program looking for Go TOs
that are to labels within the range of the code of interest.

If there are ALTERs then this is not enough because each GO TO may
have a range of targets which are not part of the GOTO itself* and
thus for each GOTO the whole program has to be examined for all the
ALTERs which reference this to find out if it could goto the range of
the code of interest.

If we strip off all the ALTERs we reduce by an order of magnitude the
search required to undestand the program.

Similarly with GOTO. If GOTOs do not exist in a program then we can
with greater confidence examine small parts of a program without
needing to understand all of it.  If gotos are only tolerated within a
defined area (such as within each section) then it is only necessary
to examine that to understand enough about the small part in question.

Being lazy I am not interested in examining any more of the source
than is absolutely necessary, so I code to suit that requirement as
best I can.


* I recall (1970?) seeing a program where the altered GO TOs had a
list of possible targets (like a GOTO DEPENDING ON without that
clause) and it would be an error to have an ALTER to one not on that
list. Checking my reference it seems that is not part of COBOL and may
have been an extension. However, I had not seen ALTER before, and once
I worked out what was happening I suggested that the program be burnt
at the stake**, the programmer was not available.

** As a total aside, IIRC in Pocklington, opposite the Black Bull pub,
there is a plaque commenorating the last public witch burning in
england.
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com...
> On Fri, 27 Jul 2007 23:42:30 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> decades old "structured programming",
[Snipped reference to EXIT PARAGRAPH, which is
irrelevant to the discussion.]

Mr Brazee, if your compiler does not have EXIT SECTION,
it may be simulated on any conforming COBOL 85 compiler.

What was described is entirely consistent with "structured
programming". Each section has a single-entry and single-exit
and each paragraph within the section has a single-entry and
single-exit.

Given that a "mini-program" may have a well-defined interface
and its functionality may be implemented either as a section or
as a paragraph group, I submit that the difference may be
characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
the "mini-program" is presented as a box made of a solid line,
and, when implemented as a paragraph group, a box made of a
dashed line.

I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
will treat each section as an entity. I chose "dashed line" to
represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
the comments that may delineate the groups.

Without the well-defined interface, one might as well surround
everything with dotted lines.

The choices are these: solid, dashed, or dotted!
```

```
My goals here are:

1.  Do not have any drop through from one paragraph to the next.
2.  Don't have a bunch of switches to emulate the exit statement.

>I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
>will treat each section as an entity. I chose "dashed line" to
>represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
>the comments that may delineate the groups.

My worry isn't the compiler, which does whatever we tell it to do - it
is the maintenance programmer, who might not notice that the paragraph
he ads can be inadvertently reached by the section above it.

To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
through process is counter to structured programming and easy
maintenance.
```

```

"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message
news:IeFri.46485$YL5.42322@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net...
> In article <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith"
<ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
> >I don't see this as realistic. When a program is built of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Isn't that part of the problem, though? One *more* thing for the
maintenance
> programmer to have to notice. Numerous studies have shown that somewhere
> around 90% of the life-cycle cost of most software is in maintenance. This
> means that when writing software, ease of maintenance should be the
paramount
> stylistic concern, head and shoulders above every other consideration.

In other words, building the program from paragraphs only
eases maintenance? I think not; but let me explain.

One of the points I have "dancing" around is the relation
between design and implementation. Faithful implementation
of a design would seem to require a style that mirrors the
style of the design. What might one do when implementing
an object-oriented design in COBOL 85, for example?

The elements of OOD include (sub)classes and methods.
Using an OOPL one would make class-names distinct and
methods non-distinct; that is, the same method-name may be
used with any (sub)class. Robert C. Martin, "Designing
Object-Oriented C++ Applications ...", suggests, indirectly,
that OOD is an inversion of procedural design. Why not,
then, invert the OOD when using procedural implementation?
That is, make method-names distinct and class-names
non-distinct.

Following is a outline of the procedural pattern for implementing
OOD in COBOL 85. I use braces to enclose the variable parts.

{method-name} section.
begin.
    evaluate class-type
        when {class-name}-id
            perform {class-name}
        ...
    end-evaluate
    exit section (or its equivalent)

{class-name}.
    {method code}.

Sections are used because class-names are non-distinct and
the compiler will resolve those names correctly. [Other
details are omitted because this is only a presentation of
a concept; not a how-to book.]

In terms of maintenance, adding a new class (which uses an
existing method-name) means making changes to the section,
and, adding a new method means adding a section. Thus
continued ease of maintenance, for this case, means adhering
to the implementation of the object-oriented design. There is,
simply, no room for mixing design methods.

The choice between sections and paragraphs-only depends
on design, continued ease of maintenance depends on the
programmer being both familiar with and faithful to the design.
To answer the question initially presented, building a program
from paragraphs only is not the only reasonable choice and
does not guarantee ease of maintenance.

> (Real-time and embedded systems are obvious exceptions, but of course
those
> would be off-topic in a Cobol group anyway. <g>)

My primary job responsibility, for more than 20 years,
was embedded systems programming. Maintaining COBOL
and C applications was secondary. Sometimes my
experience with embedded systems might spill over into
COBOL. Or, as some may say "The apple doesn't fall far
from the tree".

> >In any case, where a section includes performed paragraphs,
> >adding a paragraph means placing that paragraph within
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Sorry, but that's simply not so. When adding a paragraph to a
paragraphs-only
> program, it's impossible to *not* place it in the same program. It
certainly
> *is* possible to place a paragraph in the wrong SECTION, though. It's also
> possible to place a paragraph in the wrong *position* in the *right*
SECTION.

Novice programmer: "I changed the program like you wanted.
    I added the IF condition and the perform where you
    suggested and added the paragraph to the end of the
    source code, just like I learned. But the compiler can't
    find the paragraph!"
Senior programmer: "Didn't they teach you about nested
    programs? That program is nested! You added the
    paragraph to the wrong program!"

Many impossible things are possible given the correct set
of assumptions! <G>

> >> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
> >> through process is counter to structured programming and easy
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> even if it *is* intentional.

No! I did not say "it doesn't exist". I said it exists in
"certain special cases ...".

Calling something a "bad idea" without knowing the
circumstances for "special case" seems rather subjective
(though many other words may be used).

The special case involves loop-unrolling to increase
speed. I mentioned it because I was aware of it. If you
like, think of it as something a real-time or embedded
systems programmer might come up with when using
COBOL. <G>

When I used it, the program ran about 80% faster.
I reviewed the program about a year ago and removed
the GO TO DEPENDING choosing instead to store
additional data in each record, and with other changes
the program ran about 10% faster. I then made some
additional changes including unrolling another loop (using
repeating IF/END-IF blocks). The current program
is now running about 150% faster than when I was
using the GO TO DEPENDING.
```

```
In article <13autmji2v05a09@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:

>"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message

>eases maintenance? I think not; but let me explain.

That's part of the picture, but not all of it. Certainly, using "paragraphs 
only" is insufficient, by itself, to ease maintenance; it's also necessary to 
completely eliminate any sort of drop-through structure. Elimination of 
SECTIONs is a necessary first step to achieving this, but it's not the only 
step.
>
>One of the points I have "dancing" around is the relation
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>an object-oriented design in COBOL 85, for example?

Either transform the design to a procedural equivalent, or find a different 
langauge in which to implement it.
>

…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>a concept; not a how-to book.]

??? This would be true *if* SECTIONs had their own local data areas. They 
don't. The Data Division is global to all of them.
>

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>simply, no room for mixing design methods.

You could accomplish *exactly* the same thing with paragraphs.
>

>on design, continued ease of maintenance depends on the
>programmer being both familiar with and faithful to the design.

Some designs are inherently easier, or more difficult, to maintain than 
others. Simple familiarity and fidelity on the part of the programmer are 
insufficient.

>To answer the question initially presented, building a program
>from paragraphs only is not the only reasonable choice 

We disagree on this point.

>and does not guarantee ease of maintenance.

I certainly agree with you on this point; however, I would add the qualifier 
that *not* building from paragraphs-only *does* guarantee a design that cannot 
afford ease of maintenance.

>
>> (Real-time and embedded systems are obvious exceptions, but of course
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>from the tree".

I suspected something of the sort. Cobol's paragraph structure affords a 
richness and modularity of program control that is not available in most 
high-level languages without using functions and subroutines. Programmers 
whose experience lies mostly in languages such as C, assembler, or Fortran are 
often unprepared to write Cobol *as* Cobol because they don't *think* in 
Cobol. They think in Fortran or C or assembler, and when they write in Cobol, 
they're typically writing a Cobol translation of a Fortran or C or assembler
implementation.

And that does *not* make for good Cobol.
>
>> >In any case, where a section includes performed paragraphs,
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>of assumptions! <G>

OK, I'll give you that one -- but it's still a *lot* harder to do that, than 
to put a paragraph in the wrong SECTION.

And, of course, it is still possible to put a paragraph in the wrong place in 
the right SECTION. In a paragraphs-only program, with no drop-through control, 
that's just not an issue at all.
>
>> >> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>"certain special cases ...".

SECTIONs are *inherently* drop-through structures. PERFORMing a SECTION causes 
each paragraph in it to be executed sequentially, except as modified by GO TO, 
which is another bad idea.
>
>Calling something a "bad idea" without knowing the
>circumstances for "special case" seems rather subjective
>(though many other words may be used).

I'm going to be quite dogmatic about that, and insist that SECTIONs, 
drop-through control, and GO TO are almost always bad ideas, and that the 
special cases in which they are not are very few and very far between.

>The special case involves loop-unrolling to increase
>speed. I mentioned it because I was aware of it. If you
>like, think of it as something a real-time or embedded
>systems programmer might come up with when using
>COBOL. <G>

Exactly so. And that's the problem. :-)
>When I used it, the program ran about 80% faster.
>I reviewed the program about a year ago and removed
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T14:46:25-06:00

```

"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message
news:%JLri.1722$ox5.147@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com...
> In article <13autmji2v05a09@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith"
<ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> >
> >"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message
> >news:IeFri.46485$YL5.42322@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net...
> >> In article <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith"
> ><ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
[snip]
> >> >> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
> >> >> through process is counter to structured programming and easy
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> So because it's intentional, it doesn't exist? Drop-through is a bad
idea,
> >> even if it *is* intentional.
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> SECTIONs are *inherently* drop-through structures. PERFORMing a SECTION
causes
> each paragraph in it to be executed sequentially, except as modified by GO
TO,
> which is another bad idea.

Sections are no more "*inherently* drop-through" than
paragraphs. Performing a section (or paragraph) causes
a transfer of control to the first statement. What happens
after that depends entirely on the statements that are
executed. Specifically, there is no rule regarding sections
that requires each paragraph in a section to be executed
sequentially (The exceptions are irrelevant because the
premise is false.). The rule is that statements be executed
sequentially and that rule applies to paragraphs and
sections, equally.
```

```
On Aug 1, 4:44 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> [snip]
>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, according to you the standard is incorrect.

Where the argument is at cross-puroposes is in understanding what is
menat by each in the term 'drop-through'.

Both sections and pargraphs will drop through the lines of code within
them. In the absense of any other control a performed section will
drop-through the paragraph labels within that section (including any
paragraphs after what is intended to be the end of the section).

In the case of the structure you have said you use with:

       PERFORM ABC
       ...

    ABC SECTION.
       PERFORM A
       PERFORM B
       ....
    A.
       ...
    B.
       ...
    SomeOther SECTION.

Then the natural tendancy, in the absense of explicit control, is to
drop through A and B until SomeOther is reached (and through that too
if the SECTION word is missing).

OTOH if the word SECTION is removed from ABC then there will _not_ be
a drop through to A and B and the return will be directly from the end
of ABC _without_ any explicit control statement.

Of course you add an explicit EXIT SECTION change of control to avoid
the drop through (unless you forget it).

You seem to think that 'default' means having an 'EXIT SECTION' in the
first paragraph while what it actually means is 'without explicit
control statements', thus you fail to see the argument being made by
Doug.
```

- **From:** LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T19:39:34-06:00

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> 
> I suspected something of the sort. Cobol's paragraph structure affords a 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> And that does *not* make for good Cobol.

[snip]

>> The special case involves loop-unrolling to increase
>> speed. I mentioned it because I was aware of it. If you
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Exactly so. And that's the problem. :-)

Who is the arbiter of "good" COBOL, or whether a coding style is a 
"problem" or not?  If someone's code does what it is supposed to do, and 
they document it well, I really don't see the problem.

Take these two loops...  (FETCH is turned into a database call by a 
preprocessor, and returns its results in an alphanumeric variable called 
ERROR-NUM - "0013" means "end of chain".  If you're at the front of the 
set, feching NEXT or FIRST will give you the same record.)

[inline]
*>  Process all equipment for this unit
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET

     PERFORM UNTIL ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
          PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP
          FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET
     END-PERFORM

     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = "0013"
         GO TO ERROR-HANDLER
     END-IF.

That's the way I would write it in COBOL 85, a priming read, and one 
error check.  (Well, not in all-caps, but the logic is similar.)  Now, 
here's a common way we find them in our COBOL 74 code...

100-PROCESS-EQPP.
     FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET.

     IF ERROR-NUM = "0013"
         GO TO 100-PROCESS-EQPP-EXIT
     ELSE
         IF ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
             GO TO ERROR-HANDLER.

     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP.

     GO TO 100-PROCESS-EQPP.

100-PROCESS-EQPP-EXIT.
     EXIT.

Now, I don't particularly care for this style - but is it bad COBOL?  I 
don't know - it requires a PERFORM THRU, but it's pretty small - to mess 
this up you'd pretty much have to try to do it.  It could be written 
differently...

100-PROCESS-EQPP.
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET.

     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP
       UNTIL ERROR-NUM > ZEROES.

     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = "0013"
         GO TO ERROR-HANDLER.

This is certainly smaller, and looks more like the COBOL 85 version, but 
I don't like it.  We've now made PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP be responsible for 
getting the "next" equipment record - we've split the loop.  This 
program may even have two options - one for a specific piece of 
equipment, and another that walks all equipment for a unit.

I would postulate that the middle loop, given the constraints of COBOL 
74, works fine; although, as there are two tests of ERROR-NUM for each 
iteration, it may not be the most efficient.  And I would also postulate 
that the same code would compile and work fine on a 2002-compliant 
compiler as well.

Now, you want to talk bad COBOL - a common variant on the second loop is 
below.

100-PROCESS-EQUIP.
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-EQPP SET.
110-CONTINUE.
     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
         GO TO 199-X.
     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP.
     FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-EQPP SET.
     GO TO 110-CONTINUE.
199-X.
     EXIT.

There's an unnecessary paragraph (110-CONTINUE), they loop to the top 
before checking the error condition on the fetch, and they break the 
loop for *any* error condition, without checking for unexpected errors. 
  To me, that's bad COBOL.
```

```
> SECTION.
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…

But the point is: THE COMPILER TOLD HIM.

The whole point of _actual_ encapsulation is that the compiler can
tell you when the programmer does something wrong, as in your eaxmaple
case.

In the case of sections with non-unique paragraph names the paragraph
had been put in the wrong section it may be that the name exists in
the right pargraph and the compiler silently uses that. The result is
that after spending a week of fruitless testing where the new code
just didn't work a day is spent combing through the code to find the
problem.*

I started by saying I was lazy. I want the compiler to tell me what I
did wrong before I waste my time testing something that won't work.


* Some years ago this exact thing happened to me. SECTION headings
were still in the program, but not PERFORMed, because it had
originally been segmented (overlayed) for MS-DOS (or possibly for CP/M
or MP/M before that). A new paragraph was added that accidentally
duplicated an existing name. The PERFORM did the wrong thing
_silently_.
```

```
On Aug 1, 5:47 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> Following is a outline of the procedural pattern for implementing
> OOD in COBOL 85. I use braces to enclose the variable parts.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> a concept; not a how-to book.]

I think that you have managed to combine OO and procedural programming
in a way that eliminates all the advantages of both while retaining
all the disadvantages of each.

The whole point of 'reusable code' is that it can be reused without
copy-and-paste. You are building all the 'class' code into one
monolith by scattering their parts across all the sections while
simultaneously collecting all their data into one global block that is
Working-Storage.

If you wanted to reuse a 'class' in another program would you go
through picking out all the parts individually and adding the WHEN
clauses ?

The whole point of OOD is that it is 'O'rientated to the 'O'bject. All
the parts that relate to a class are collected together while you have
scattered them.

Implementing a subset of OOD in Cobol-85 is already catered for with
sub-programming. Granted it is necessary to pass a 'method' parameter
into the separate program that is called for each 'class'.

> My primary job responsibility, for more than 20
> years, was embedded systems programming.

That probably explains why you build single huge monolith programs
that accesses a global data area. The overhead of using CALL of
separate code pieces that were _actually_ encapsulated would be
abhorent.
```

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com> <13asanp7chflce6@corp.supernews.com>`

```
On Jul 31, 6:14 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> Mr Brazee, if your compiler does not have EXIT SECTION,
> it may be simulated on any conforming COBOL 85 compiler.

By a GO TO ?

> Given that a "mini-program" may have a well-defined interface
> and its functionality may be implemented either as a section or
> as a paragraph group,

Neither are actually a 'mini-program'. For example they have no data
areas, those are still in the W-S and accessible to all other blocks
of code should they choose to do so. They are merely blocks of code.
It may well be that the program structure is such that they are
clearly isolated and the way of using them is defined.

> I submit that the difference may be
> characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
> the "mini-program" is presented as a box made of a solid line,
> and, when implemented as a paragraph group, a box made of a
> dashed line.

Excuse me, but what exactly is drawing these 'lines', do you have a
special editing and/or printing function ? Or is is just your
imagination ? The compiler makes no such distinction, there is no
difference at all between a 'solid line' and a 'dotted line' in the
language.

> I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> will treat each section as an entity.

No it won't.

> I chose "dashed line" to
> represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
> the comments that may delineate the groups.

> Without the well-defined interface, one might as well surround
> everything with dotted lines.

You are confusing two quite distinct things. Yes, well defined
interfaces are a good thing. Yes, breaking down procedures into
smaller parts (paragraphs) is a good thing. But sticking on a SECTION
word while doing this is irrelevant, it adds nothing (except the
opportunity to do a GO TO). It does not do any actual 'encapsulation'
beyond catering for duplicate labels (which is not necessarily a good
thing).

> The choices are these: solid, dashed, or dotted!

If you want actual encapsulation and interfaces that are enforced
then:

Solid line = objects
Dashed line = separate programs
dotted line = sub-programs

sections don't even get a rating.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **In-Reply-To:** `<13at87dgirq9jcc@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com> <13asanp7chflce6@corp.supernews.com> <1185835364.404470.56690@m37g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13at87dgirq9jcc@corp.supernews.com>`

```
On Jul 31, 2:37 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

> > > I submit that the difference may be
> > > characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> graphically as".

I am characterizing it as blank line.

> > > I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> > > will treat each section as an entity.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> meaningless.

No. SECTIONs may be the basis of segmentation. Or they may be the
basis of a hierarchical program structure with a sub-structure of
paragraphs. Neither requires they be treated as an 'entity' any more
so than a paragraph is or a statement is. _You_ may be treating them
as an 'entity' with special privileges and attributes but the compiler
does not.

They _would_ be useful if there were some way to get the compiler to
complain if something outside the section performed a paragraph or
ensured that within a section only local paragraphs could be performed
or local data could be defined. In fact that is what sub-programs can
do, they are actually encapsulated, actually an entity.

> > You are confusing two quite distinct things. Yes, well defined
> > interfaces are a good thing. Yes, breaking down procedures into
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>     A. GO TO A.

OK, I'll correct myself: it adds the opportunity to do a GO TO to a
different paragraph within the section.

Whereas, for example:

        PERFORM ABC

    ABC-Header SECTION.    < or simply a comment
    ABC.
       PERFORM somepartofABC
       PERFORM anotherpartofABC
       .

    somepartofABC.
       ...
       ...
    anotherpartofABC.
       ...

does not add such an opportunity but is just as "mini-programish",
just as much an 'entity'
```

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185865451.293160.20430@j4g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> On Jul 31, 2:37 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
[snip]
> > > > I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> > > > will treat each section as an entity.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> does not.

You seem to expressing your view of what an entity is
(or is not) and not what it is (or is not) to the compiler,
then you mistakenly attribute your view to that of the
compiler. This appears in "Neither requires they be
treated as an 'entity' ...", while the rules for COBOL
require that section headers be recognized and the
section and its contents be treated according to those
rules. [entity: being or existence, esp. when considered
as distinct or self-contained] A COBOL compiler will
recognize the existence of each section as something
distinct from all other sections.
```

```
On Aug 1, 3:41 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> > Fine -- it is neither distinct nor self-contained. All data is global, and
> > any
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> depends on the way the person writing the program has coded it).

But it is no more 'distinct' or 'self-contained' than similarly
constructed code _without_ the word SECTION (except as I pointed out
before the section allows for GOTO ~exit).

> You are also right that any paragraph within the SECTION can be PERFORMED
> from anywhere, but that is part of what is leading to the debate here.

Exactly, the claims that it is 'encapsulated' in some way by having
the word SECTION is misleading.

> If you consider a section to be a paragraph, then your comments and position
> are perfectly correct.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> SECTION to be a paragraph (amongst other paragraphs)..

You are using the words 'section' and 'paragraph' in ways that lead to
confusion. I don't know what you mean by "considers a SECTION to be a
paragraph". SECTIONs have the word SECTION, paragraphs don't.

> If one doesn't, then there are actually some benefits available from
> SECTIONed code.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the start), then most of the objections you have evaporate.

You should note that Rick specifically uses performed paragraphs
within the performed section in the mistaken expectation that this
makes it 'encapsulated'.

> You CAN'T use GO TOs or PERFORMs from inside or outside of it, so that looks
> like a step in the right direction. But, hang on a minute, then what you
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> simply a matter of what you prefer. Style. Pure and simple.

Actually there is an real difference. Certainly if all labels have the
word SECTION and there are no paragraph labels (there should be no
need for a dummy paragraph at the start and no exit paragraphs) then
there is no logical difference between that and the same program with
all the word SECTION removed.

The issue then is how can we be assured that this is the case. It
would be an error if one label was wrong by either missing its word
SECTION or having one spuriously, an error that may not cause enough
of a problem to be noticed in testing.

In the case of 'paragraph only' it is a simple grep or text search to
guarantee that the convention has been correctly followed. In the case
of 'section only' each label must be examined. I am lazy and choose
the easy way.

> I use SECTIONs when coding COBOL much the same way as I use an outline view
> when writing an article or paper. It gives me a framework to hang the big
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> them so I can test the flow of control as it develops.

Once you get over the 'I need an exit' habit (which _is_ a paragraph
within the section) then it can be seen that breaking down into
smaller blocks can be an advantage and the SECTION word is just
clutter.
```

```
Further to that another area is if each label is supposed to be a
SECTION header.

If you have code that is
   MAIN SECTION.

    PERFORM A THRU A-exit
    ..

   A SECTION.
     ...
        GO TO A-exit
     ...
   A-exit SECTION.
     EXIT.
   B.

Then the A-exit section includes the paragraph B so this is part of
the perform scope.
```

## Thread: Tracing paragraph Names in Pro Cobol — 12 this month / 22 total

### Tracing paragraph Names in Pro Cobol

- **From:** balaji.amarnath@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2007-07-25T22:35:08-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185428108.957939.304810@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
Dear All,
        Is there anyway of tracing a paragraph name in a cobol program
in runtime.
        I am using procobol of MicroFocus.
        I want to trace a paragraph where there was a error (Both Run
TIme and Logical error).
       Have a Great Day.

Thanks and Regards
Balaji
```

#### ↳ Re: Tracing paragraph Names in Pro Cobol

- **From:** "Steve Rainbird" <news.nospam@rainbird.me.nospam.uk>
- **Date:** 2007-07-26T09:54:43+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5gr5q6F3hj156U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185428108.957939.304810@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
<balaji.amarnath@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:1185428108.957939.304810@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> Dear All,
>        Is there anyway of tracing a paragraph name in a cobol program
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>


Add $SET TRACE at the beginning of your program.

Then put READY TRACE where you want tithe trace to start.  It will show you 
what paragraph names are being performed.
```

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:f8cdu0$iqf$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <1185510901.109004.217150@i38g2000prf.googlegroups.com>,
> <balaji.amarnath@gmail.com> wrote:
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> debugging technique over two-and-a-half decades old?

Funny, I had exactly that same thought... visions of a 360 where we coded 
stuff like...

IF UPSI-8-ON
     READY TRACE.

... at the start of every main program, and there  was a SOP that if 
something crashed the Operator set User Program Switch Indicator number 8 ON 
(it was technically 7 as they started at 0, but they replaced what had been 
8 physical toggle switches on the 1401 [ "sense switches" A through G, 
referred to on our site as "1 through 8"]), re-ran the job, and then 
provided the trace to the programmers in the morning...

Fortunately, things have moved on a bit since 1967, in both the mainframe 
and network arenas.

I have an old copy of MicroFocus ProCOBOL on a Windows 3.1 machine 
somewhere. I seem to recall that it had Animation even way back then... 
(could be wrong; I haven't used it for many years).

What I'd really like to know is why someone would post and suggest "READY 
TRACE", but NOT mention "RESET TRACE"... :-)

I guess, given what the original question was, it is a fair response; it 
just seemed incomplete.

Sometimes the right question is every bit as important as the right answer.

Pete.
```

## Thread: Question regarding uniqueness — 23 msgs

### Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** "Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T13:48:27+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. t11.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.

01 K525-AII-DRVR-RSTRT-DATA.
  03 VARIABLE1 PIC X(8) VALUE SPACES.
  03 K525-FRMT-FILE-ARRAY.
      06  K525-FRMT-FILE-ARRAY-ROW
          OCCURS 6 TIMES
         ASCENDING KEY IS VARIABLE1.
       09 VARIABLE1 PIC X(8) VALUE SPACES.
       09 OTHER-DATA-ITEM PIC S9(5).
PROCEDURE DIVISION.
GOBACK.

This compiles with big iron and MF.
Why?
Of course, this is a stupid definition.
Any attempt in the PROC div to reference any field
would lead to a compile failure.
OC throws this out at compile time.
```

#### ↳ Re: Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T07:56:16-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<K3mqi.114$591.112@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
"Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote in message 
news:f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com...
> IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
> PROGRAM-ID. t11.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Why?

Why not?  the ASCENDING KEY clause must specify datanames which are part of 
the same group item, so *in context* VARIABLE1 is non-ambiguous.

Methinks you'd have some problems if you referred to VARIABLE1 anywhere in 
the PROCEDURE DIVISION, but I don't see a problem just compiling the DATA 
DIVISION.

MCM
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T13:29:43+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5rmqi.965$mk7.525@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net>`
- **References:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com> <K3mqi.114$591.112@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
In article <K3mqi.114$591.112@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:
>"Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote in message 
>news:f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com...
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>the same group item, so *in context* VARIABLE1 is non-ambiguous.

The problem is the line right after that: duplicate definitions of VARIABLE1. 

The reference VARIABLE1 OF K525-FRMT-FILE-ARRAY OF K525-AII-DRVR-RSTRT-DATA is 
not ambiguous, but the reference VARIABLE1 OF K525-AII-DRVR-RSTRT-DATA *is*, 
and thus the duplication should cause a compile-time error. It's an illegal 
construct, regardless of whether it's referenced in the Procedure Division or 
not.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T09:04:03-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<FXmqi.130$ER7.88@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com> <H0mqi.26842$bz7.24027@newssvr22.news.prodigy.net>`

```
"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message 
news:H0mqi.26842$bz7.24027@newssvr22.news.prodigy.net...
> Because their parsers are broken.
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> As well it should. As should every Cobol compiler.

I am afraid you are incorrect, sir.

Both VARIABLE1 data items are subordinate to group items, and therefore may 
be qualified within the procedure division to eliminate ambiguity.

>>01 K525-AII-DRVR-RSTRT-DATA.
>>  03 VARIABLE1 PIC X(8) VALUE SPACES.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>>       09 OTHER-DATA-ITEM PIC S9(5).


The first reference is VARIABLE1 OF K525-ALL-DRVR-RSTST-DATA, the second is 
VARIABLE1 OF K525-FRMT-FILE-ARRAY-ROW (subscript).


MCM
```

```
In article <FXmqi.130$ER7.88@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:

I disagree. :-)
>
>Both VARIABLE1 data items are subordinate to group items, and therefore may 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>VARIABLE1 OF K525-FRMT-FILE-ARRAY-ROW (subscript).

Right -- and since the ASCENDING KEY phrase doesn't include *either* 
qualifier, that's an ambiguous reference. The compiler should flag either 
that, or the second instance of VARIABLE1, but to leave them both unflagged is 
incorrect behavior.
```

- **References:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com>`

```

"Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de> wrote in message
news:f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com...
> IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
> PROGRAM-ID. t11.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Why?

A data-name need not be unique unless it is referenced.

Current rules are at FDIS ISO/IEC 1989:2002 page 83,
8.4.1 Uniqueness of reference,
"Every user-defined name in a source element is assigned, by
the user, to name a resource that is to be used in solving a
data processing problem. (See 8.3.1.1.1, User-defined words.)
In order to use a resource, a statement shall contain a reference
that uniquely identifies that resource. In order to ensure
uniqueness of reference, a user-defined name may be qualified,
subscripted, or reference modified as described in the following
paragraphs."

One question that I considered is whether "VARIABLE1" in
the KEY phrase is unique, without explicit qualification.
According to the OCCURS clause, SR(3), this use of
"VARIABLE1" meets the requirement that it "shall be the name
of either the entry containing the OCCURS clause or an entry
subordinate to the entry containing the OCCURS clause" and,
therefore, need not be explicitly qualified; though it may be so
qualified under SR(2).

> Of course, this is a stupid definition.
> Any attempt in the PROC div to reference any field
> would lead to a compile failure.

Not according to my tests with Micro Focus COBOL 3.2.
These statements work when inserted into the given code.
-----
           move "a" to variable1 of k525-frmt-file-array-row (1)
           display variable1 of k525-frmt-file-array-row (1)
-----
That is, these statements appear to meet the current standard
as well as the rules published in the LRM for that compiler.
```

#### ↳ Re: Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-28T03:25:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<yGyqi.194943$%85.33057@fe05.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<f8cm2c$vpa$00$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
I believe that any compiler conforming to the '85 Standard needs to compile this 
cleanly.  See page XV88-42, which states,

"(8) Uniaueness of reference (1 NUC). A user-defined word need not be unique or 
be capable of being made unique unless referenced."

This was a substantive change from the '74 Standard.  (The reason that it was 
done - as I recall it - was to allow for COPY members with non-unique, not 
possible of being mad eunique, but unreferenced data items).

There certainly MIGHT be a question about whenther
  "ASCENDING KEY IS VARIABLE1"

is a reference to VARIABLE1, but I do not believe that such uses are considered 
a "reference" in COBOL syntax.
```

#### ↳ Re: Question regarding uniqueness

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>

```
You can ignore my last post (which was a different change).  The change that 
relates to this is in the '02 Standard, page 827 which states,

"67) Implicit qualification of data-names within the same group. Data-names 
referenced in a data description entry need not be qualified when they are 
defined in the same group as the subject of the entry, unless qualifiers are 
needed to establish uniqueness within that group."
```
